B005HV6Y5W|3.0|With Mixed Results.<br /><br />***This review may contain spoilers**<br /><br />Finally finding the leisure time and inclination, I watched \\"The Tree of Life\\" (hereafter ToL) on HBO on Sunday, July 8, 2012 at 9:40 a.m.  At the end, I concluded that if you 1) like going to church, 2) like thinking about the movies you watch and/or 3) don't mind following a convoluted non-linear plot, then \\"Tol\\" should satisfy you.  If you don't like doing any of the aforementioned things, then \\"ToL\\" will probably be a boring slog.<br /><br />I agree with the posters who advise viewers to watch \\"ToL\\" with a calm, contemplative attitude, because \\"Tol\\" indeed is not your conventional fun time at the movies.  The director Terrence Malick recklessly and audaciously is swinging for the spiritual fences and is daring to ask the Big Questions about morality, God's justice (or lack thereof), man's relationship to God, why even good people suffer, all that deep stuff.  However, Malick collides with the inherent, unavoidable problem any director challenging the infinite has: his literal, visual interpretation of God's mind is necessarily, pretentiously inadequate.  I am referring to the admittedly spectacular biological, psychological, paleontological, and cosmic images wafting through \\"ToL\\".  The quiet images, by themselves, announce to those who are receptive that, yeah, God is great and majestic.  No real problem there, even if time-worn, if Malick had left it at that and created a balanced version of \\"Koyaanisqatsi\\", the 80's film that used human images to illustrate life's chaos.<br /><br />However, Malick artificially grafts this \\"divine imagery\\" to an earthy, earthly tale of the chronological history of a `50s Texas family headed by a somewhat disciplinarian, occupationally frustrated father (Brad Pitt) and gentle, compassionate mother (Jessica Chastain).  Respectively, they personify the perennial nature vs. nurture conflict (I prefer the word \\"nurture\\" to the vague term \\"grace\\").  Knowing them only by their last names Mr. and Mrs. O'Brien solidifies their symbolic status.  The true conflict revolves around the festering rivalry between the father and his oldest son Jack (Hunter McCracken as a boy, Sean Penn as an adult).  The languid, usually silent, and twisty progression of the family's life, beginning toward the end when it receives a telegram informing them of the death of, I assume, the second oldest brother as a (I think) Vietnam soldier, and the events that test their mettle and their faith, is also compelling, again by itself.<br /><br />But when \\"ToL\\" attempts to meld these two tropes, the mundane and the ethereal, I'm afraid it dilutes the power of both tropes.  I think I see the movie's purpose here; to show that God transcends mere earthly affairs and that the power of caring, of love, of nurture will quash the woes and cares of brute animal nature and will bring us peace, at least in the afterworld.  Again, it's a noble purpose that few movies dare touch.  But, you know, the result comes across as preachy and simplistic and as platitudinous as the well-meaning but hackneyed words of comfort the Grandmother (Fiona Shaw) gives Mrs. O'Brien after she gets that telegram.<br /><br />If Malick had exclusively taken the \\"2001/Koyaanisqatsi\\" route or \\"Great Santini\\" route, personally I would have more impressed.  By combining them in \\"ToL\\" it makes the effort seem a little self-conscious and forced.  Lovely and stunning as the \\"cosmic\\" images were, I would have excised them and kept the remaining family structure, even the dreamy, stream-of-consciousness elements intact.  That way, we could see the O'Brien family legitimately wrestle with their psychosocial and spiritual dilemmas (especially Jack's concern that he is emulating the less admirable aspects of his father's character) and arrive (or not arrive) at a resolution.  Frankly, the cosmic images are, I think, a tad artistically dishonest, because we the audience can witness them and perhaps take solace from them, while the mundane O'Briens cannot.  Too dues ex machina for my taste.<br /><br />Therefore, my verdict on \\"ToL\\" is ambiguous.  I aesthetically appreciated Malick's attempt at apotheosis, to remind us that love is the path to God.  But my intellect recoiled at the clich&eacute;d imagery \\"ToL\\" resorted to in order to demonstrate this, even if the movie had to.  But I can just envision posters telling me, \\"Don't think about the movie too much, just feel it.\\"  I suppose.  If you just let \\"ToL\\"s flow bear you along, it may make you feel better.  Very well - 3.5 stars out of 5.<br /><br />P.S.: Two trivial points - first, the plesiosaur depicted in the movie could never had curled its neck backwards the way it did.  Fossil studies show it lacked that flexibility.  Second, the raptor shown later in the movie probably spared the prostrate hadrosaur not because it was merciful, but because it was not hungry.haps take solace from them, while the mundane O'Briens cannot.  Too dues ex machina for my taste.<br /><br />Therefore, my verdict on \\"ToL\\" is ambiguous.  I aesthetically appreciated Malick's attempt at apotheosis, to remind us that love is the path to God. Fossil studies show it lacked that flexibility.  Second, the raptor shown later in the movie probably spared the prostrate hadrosaur not because it was merciful, but because it was not hungry.

B007HC3LHS|5.0|Ever since viewing Hitchcock's \\"Shadow of a Doubt\\" where a young girl suspects that the uncle everyone so admires is in reality a serial killer, I have thought that the most frightening psychological scenario is one where the victim is the only one who can see that the Emperor has no clothing, yet in this case, that there is a monster under the charm.  When she tries to get help in order to escape from his destructiveness, no one believes her because the narcissistic perpetrator has already effectively manipulated her potential saviors into believing she is mentally unstable & cannot be believed (think \\"Gaslight\\").  This is the story (which we have read before) of a young woman coming out of denial and finding herself involved with a malignant narcissist only to discover that all her allies have been enchanted by the monster's charisma & she has nowhere to turn.  What makes this story more compelling than most in this genre is the structure of the novel: alternating between journal entries four years apart so the reader both sees the perpetrator's slow acquisition of control as well as the victim's growing terror and then the sequelae of her traumatic experience.  It reminds me some of \\"Still Missing\\" by Chevy Stevens because both books focus as much on the aftermath of an abduction/domestic violence than on the trauma itself.  If you like psychological suspense novels where you are both fascinated and simultaneously terrified to see what happens next, you are likely to find this debut novel hard to put done.

B006PTL1QM|4.0|David Cronenberg likes to take on sexually and violently charged controversies, so it's not surprising that he was attracted by Christopher Hampton's play \\"The Talking Cure\\", whose storyline was based on the book \\"A Most Dangerous Method\\" by John Kerr, an exploration of the role that Russian psychoanalyst Sabina Spielrein played in the relationship between Sigmund Freud and Carl Jung in the years before World War I. Christopher Hampton wrote the screenplay for \\"A Dangerous Method\\" as well, and that is evident in the strong dialogue.<br /><br />In 1904, Sabina Spielrein (Keira Knightley), daughter of wealthy Russian Jews, is brought to the Burgh&ouml;lzhi Clinic in Zurich in a state of hysteria. Dr. Carl Jung (Michael Fassbender) wants to try the theoretical \\"talking cure\\" developed by Austrian psychoanalyst Sigmund Freud (Viggo Mortensen) on her. Conveniently, Miss Spielrein turns out to be a masochist who is sexually excited by humiliation. Sabina recovers and enrolls at university with the intention of becoming a psychoanalyst herself. But her aggressive, raw sexuality proves too much for Carl Jung to resist, which eventually threatens his social standing and his already ideologically strained relationship with Freud.<br /><br />I was surprised that I liked this film as much as I did, because I have never found the work of either Freud or Jung the least bit interesting. I find them both largely fanciful. But there is more insight into the human psyche to be found in the ideological and personal conflicts between Freud and Jung than in anything those men published. There is real drama in their creeping hatred for one another. Freud's insistence on an exclusively sexual interpretation of clinical material versus Jung's increasingly far flung interests in parapsychology. This is exacerbated by conflicts of class, ethnicity, and personality. In walks Sabina Spielrein to stir it all up, in love with Jung but usually siding with Freud.<br /><br />I'm not sure if Keira Knightley overacted Sabina Spielrein's illness, but she deftly conveys Sabina's bright, self-possessed, but slightly nutty character for most of the film. Viggo Mortensen is unrecognizable as Sigmund Freud. It's not just the make-up. His voice is unlike I've ever heard it before. Jung is perhaps the trickiest of the trio to portray, as he is an affluent, respectable WASP, with wealthy wife and beautiful children, who, in spite of his criticism of Freud's practicality, is the most practical of all. He mistreats his mistress to protect his fortune and social standing. Both this production and the Dresden production are quasi-modern, and both has their pros and cons in terms of staging.<br />Focusing on the music and singing, both Nello Santi and Fabio Luisi would justly be said to represent the old and new Italian operatic conducting school. Both give authentic readings of the famous score.<br />For the vocal acting side, the two casts are also fairly even.  Leo Nucci has the clear visual edge, being more slightly built and with vivid acting skills both vocally and dramatically. While he could not be said to be in the same vocal prime stage as Lucic, Nucci has the experience and skill to balance out any vocal shortcomings that beset his age. B0011Z32PS|5.0|When Metallica's BLACK album was released, I bought the casette tape. It stayed in my car's deck my entire freshman year of college.  Like a lot of Metallica's music, the songs unfold over time.  Meaning the music is accessible, but dense, and you'll get a lot of nuances out of it on, say the hundredth time you listen to it that you might not have heard the first dozen.<br /><br />Because this albums was the first that had a couple songs make it onto some top 40 charts, a lot of metalheads disparage this album. But one thing to keep in mind is that the early 90's was a brief period of time in recent American music history when Grunge and Hard Rock and music with distortion and anger was actually popular.  The same thing happened to Iron Maiden's SEVENTH SON OF A SEVENTH SON.  \\"Moonchild\\" made it onto the charts, and all of a sudden there was this outrage amongst Maiden fans.  My point is that the BLACK album is a solid metal album that doesn't pull any punches.  If it came out today, I'd imagine it wouldn't get the same fanfare because, let's face it, popular music has gone soft. (Don't get me started).<br /><br />That being said, a lot of people suggest this album as a 1st Metallica album, seeing it as more accessible than their earlier stuff.  I'd disagree. A good analogy would be which Led Zeppelin album to buy first. Yeah, you can go with Led Zeppelin 4.  I mean, it's packed with iconic hit songs like Black Dog, Rock n Roll, Four Sticks and Stairway to Heaven, but really Led Zeppelin 3 is the album to begin with. It's a little rawer and less produced.  And because it doesn't have songs you've heard too much on the radio, it's easier to experience as an album rather than gravitating to the songs with which you may already be familiar.  If you're thinking about getting into a band and really trying to understand what they're musically trying to do- grab an album that's more or less a clean palate.  Similarly, RIDE THE LIGHTNING or MASTER OF PUPPETS is the place to begin with Metallica.  Sure, you may have heard a few songs, but not to the point where you're worked on any one of them. Trust me.  Both albums are masterpieces and worth listening to an infinite number of times.begin with Metallica. B007I2BZIE|5.0|This CD requires several listening sessions before formulating an entire opinion. Initially, I was a little mixed about it but after listening multiple times, I have come to view it as one of Rush's best studio works in quite a while. Certainly, new fans will appreciate the musical talent of Rush as a 3-member act with this work but true, longtime, impartial Rush fans will also recognize that Lee/Lifeson/Peart are still at the \\"top of their game\\".<br /><br />I had read a few reviews mentioning how Geddy Lee can no longer sing in the same range as he used to and also how the mixed result of this CD sounded as if it was pieced together. While it is probably true that Geddy's voice is not the same as 30 years ago, the same can be said for Bono, Jon Anderson, Robert Plant and anyone else who had an exceptional range. Still-in-all, Geddy's voice is strong in this work and the musical synergy of the trio more than makes up for any \\"perceived\\" loss of range. One is too busy listening and enjoying than worrying about what keys that Rush is playing in to accommodate Geddy's vocal range.<br /><br />I listened on both home/car stereos and could not tell where the music was heard as being \\"pieced\\" together. Frankly, I was too busy focusing on the music, which is a 12-song effort. Also, perhaps I am just not that adept at being able to tell the difference at whether or not Geddy and Alex were in the studio at the same time that Neil was recording his tracks. In essence, who cares? The music is definitely seamless.<br /><br />My initial reaction about the musicianship was that it was stellar -- among the best that Rush has ever done. Neil Peart introduces some new drum rhythms, Geddy's bass playing is phenomenal and Alex Lifeson incorporates new ideas, new textures and new sounds that he hasn't really used before. One gets a sense of the cohesion and chemistry that Rush has enjoyed over the last 40 years of making music together. It is a rarity and one that has put them on a well-deserved high pedestal in the world of progressive rock. Surprisingly, many songs on this CD have a harder \\"drive\\" and, in general, are more hard-rocking compared to some of Rush's past work... Not bad for the three, who each have to be close to 60 years old.<br /><br />I won't go into what each song means. Just know that it's Professor Peart, hard at work, waxing poetic about the purported theme of the album:<br /><br />\\"In a young man's quest to follow his dreams, he is caught between the grandiose forces of order and chaos. He travels across a lavish and colorful world of steampunk and alchemy, with lost cities, pirates, anarchists, exotic carnivals, and a rigid Watchmaker who imposes precision on every aspect of daily life.\\"<br /><br />Each song on this CD is catchy in its own respect but some favorites here are \\"The Anarchist\\", \\"Carnies\\", \\"Seven Cities of Gold\\", \\"The Wreckers\\", \\"Headlong Flight\\" and \\"The Garden\\". \\"The Garden\\" has a softer, ballad-like approach and demonstrates the vastness of creativity in Rush. In fact, \\"The Garden\\" is a significant achievement for them in their own right -- it juxtaposes the rest of the hard-rocking album with a final, softer, more humanistic song, replete with string ensemble, about the fragility of life and how easily personal achievements are lost, from one moment of being to the next. When I hear this song and compare it to the entire history of Rush, it marks a particular high maturity and importance...<br /><br />The more I listen to this CD, the more hooked I am. Although I saw merit in \\"Vapor Trails\\" and \\"Snakes and Arrows\\" (as I do in all of Rush's music), \\"Clockwork Angels\\" has so much more going on musically. There are so many new ideas incorporated that I find myself constantly vascillating between the heavy, rapid pounding beat of \\"Headlong Flight\\" and the ballad-like, well-orchestrated \\"The Garden\\" in my daily activities... \\"Headlong Flight\\" has the tempo of \\"Bastille Day\\" (Fly by Night) and \\"Analog Kid\\" (Signals), and (to me) is borderline metal. The same applies to much of Alex's playing on CA and it is really refreshing to see how Rush has evolved as a band. By the same token, they have put a lot of musical effort into this work and should be given proper due. They could have packed it in and retired many times in the past 10-15 years but they have taken the risks and surpassed them by far.<br /><br />Those who are still living in Rush's past days (e.g. Tom Sawyer, Subdivisions), as some reviewers seem to be, will not appreciate this work as much as those who can objectively listen. The musical architecture of \\"Clockwork Angels\\" reflects three legendary musicians with extraordinary musical vision, who \\"march to the beat\\" of their own music...<br /><br />On a final note, the use of keyboards is also reintroduced on this work in a tasteful, but not overbearing manner. Overall, Rush has put together a very formidable, respectable effort on \\"Clockwork Angels\\".ls), and (to me) is borderline metal. This is not to say that a world run by children would be better, but the dichotomy between our world and Ender's is that the power and influence children have is not completely ignored. Adults still have their deception, but so do Ender and his friends. They cheat the system, so he does too.<br /><br />This social commentary doesn't end with mere age and ideological differences, though, as the story also examines political and intellectual strata. The characters of Demosthenes and Locke are prime examples of all of these. From my point of view, intelligence is severely undervalued in our society, and Ender's Game brings this to light.<br /><br />There are so many profound motifs in this book that I'm not even going to try to cover them, but this is just to give the scope of the brilliance of this novel.<br /><br />Recently I decided to reinvigorate my library by having a Science Fiction literature renaissance, and Ender's Game was the perfect beginning for this goal. I highly recommend this book if you enjoy traditional science fiction adventures with sociopolitical and intellectual intrigue, especially if you are considering reviving your own childhood. Yes, I said \\"reviving\\", and I mean it. To say the least, this book affected me in many positive ways.<br /><br />Give this book a read and a thought, and it will provide for you a powerful memory - one that I believe I will never give up.

But since it is the most entertaining John Grisham novel I've read in years, I decided to bump it up to 4 stars rather than bump it down to 3 stars.<br /><br />Oscar Finley and Wally Figg are the only 2 partners of a two-bit law firm in Chicago who specialize in quickie divorces, DUIs, personal injury, and an assortment of other cases that are basically the bottom-dwellers of the law profession.  Oscar, a sixty-something grouch trapped in an unhappy marriage, spends most of his time counting the days until he can retire; while Wally, a forty-something recovering alcoholic, is forever coming up with schemes that he says will make them millionaires.  Then, one day, one of Wally's clients tells him about this drug that is supposedly causing people to die of heart-attacks; and suddenly, Wally's dream of riches and corporate jets does not seem so far-fetched anymore.  Armed with nothing but the testimony of a half dozen unreliable witnesses and a couple of pages of research done on the internet, Wally files suit against one of the largest pharmaceutical companies in America; and thus begins the hilarious story of 3 men's attempt to milk the justice system for a quick buck.  Joining Oscar and Wally on this misconceived quest is David Zinc, a 31-year-old Harvard-educated lawyer already burnt-out by the daily grind of pushing papers for a large law firm.  And when the pharmaceutical company choose to not settle like the lawyers of Finley & Figg expect them to, our erstwhile \\"heroes\\" has no choice but to go to court and fight till the end, nevermind that none of them knows the first thing about trial litigation.<br /><br />This is definitely not your typical John Grisham thriller. Instead of a hero who's fighting to right a grave injustice, you've got two sleaze-ball lawyers out to make a quick buck.  The author was careful never to take anything too seriously, so the result is a funny book that manages to gently mock the litigious society we've become without crossing the line into being sanctimonious.  Both Wally and Oscar are amoral but essentially harmless, so I found myself laughing out loud at many of their antics and their bumbling attempts to convince the judge, and plaintiff, and the cutthroat attorneys the plaintiff has hired that they actually have a case.  Interspersed with the shenanigans in the courtroom are the equally hilarious hanky-pankies that go on in their personal lives, which added an additional layer of comedy to an already vastly entertaining book.<br /><br />What kept this from being a 5 star or even a solid 4 star book for me is the predictability of the ending.  The absence of a hero that you can root for also detracted from the enjoyment somewhat, although David Zinc did his best to be a straight-man's foil to Wally and Oscar's inept villainy.  Still, I enjoyed this book tremendously and would recommend it to anyone who's got hankering for a legal thriller with a heavy dose of the absurd.
0060936223|3.0|Any book that mentions Jonathan Richman (as a drummer!) has something going for it.  Overall, I enjoyed the book as an artist's odyssey and chronicle of a deep and supportive friendship.  However, the endless lists of poetry openings, and clothing choices for events grew tedious in the latter third of the book.  Similarly, the lists of minor literary and cultural-scene figures of the time don't carry much weight today.<br /><br />Yet, those folks did create a fecund art scene for a few years that has retained its impact on art and culture.  What really drew me to the book was learning about that time and place when it came together.  As a bonus, we hear about it from the perspective of a genuinely starving artist.  The chapters about life in the Chelsea Hotel are wonderful, and anyone who has artistic sensibility would wish that he or she had the nerve of Smith and Mapplethorpe to drop into that milieu.  Smith really makes you understand what it would be like to be a relatively normal person who is befriended by Allen Ginsberg and William Burroughs, and who meets Janis Joplin (a few times) and Jimi Hendrix (briefly).<br /><br />Just as importantly, Smith explains how to become an artist.  In a phrase, it's hard work.  She and Mapplethorpe spent endless hours drawing, painting, reading (not Mapplethorpe), scouting around town for ideas, discussing aesthetics and philosophy, listening to music of all types, and more.  The intensity of their work time and artistic explorations is remarkable, and it's a lesson for today's budding artists who might be looking for a shortcut in our rapid-media age.  The depth of their artistic quest should embarrass anyone who thinks that their YouTube riff is art.<br /><br />While I'm not sure it's actually plausible, think about the routine that Smith said she lived for a couple of years.  Get up at dawn, and work in a bookstore all day (she says she left the Chelsea at 7 a.m. to open the store), and arrive home at about 7 p.m.  Grab whatever food you can afford, perhaps made on a hot plate in your one room in the hotel.  Then run down to Max's, and hang out all night.  Hard to believe she could do that even twice a week, not to mention every night (which is her claim in the book).  On the other hand, lots of us enjoy feeling that we have a dual life, and we put in great amounts of energy to both make a living and retain some type of artistic inner life.<br /><br />A final note.  During the week I read this book, my wife and I started watching the TV show \\"Wonder Years\\" on Netflix with our son.  The show focuses on a 12-year-old boy growing up on Long Island in 1968, and then moves forward with him for a few years.  Patti Smith moved to New York without a dollar to her name in July 1967, and the seminal events in the book take place in the next 3-4 years.  In other words, she was living 20 miles from the fictional \\"Kevin\\" in \\"Wonder Years\\" at the same time.  She lived in the world that was changing; he watched it from afar.  Ironically, he actually drew from a wider and more contemporary range of material because she, as an artist, ignored much of the culture that was around her.  And both were deeply changed by the times in which they lived.

0765316994|4.0|This is the fourth Scalzi I've read, and Scalzi proves again that he has a talent for dialogue, for character, and for crafting a clever plot in a bite size portion.<br /><br />While I would never call myself a Trekkie--I much prefer Star Wars, and I have never been able to buy into Roddenberry's vision of a utopian future where mankind has solved it's scarcity problems and given up money, but I digress--I've watched enough to know that the guys who die are almost always--always--the guys in the red shirts. They're unnamed extras, low level members of Star Fleet that die to provide some level of tension to the episode. And this book is through their eyes, through the eyes of the red shirted members of \\"away teams\\" that die at the first sign of danger.<br /><br />I picked up Redshirts because it looked clever, but I didn't expect any amount of depth. In fact, Scalzi's plot begins deceptively simple. He develops his red shirted characters with heavy use of dialogue that is fast and flowing, typical Scalzi, that is more difficult than it looks. That's part of what make's Scalzi so good.<br /><br />However, for the most part, the book starts with a gimmick, and rides that gimmick, for almost three-quarters through the novel. Then, without giving up any spoilers, a shift occurs, and suddenly the writing is serious, smart, and insightful, more a paean to writing than science fiction.<br /><br />And perhaps it's better I leave it there. The ending, the shift, was what made the book a four out of five stars instead of just three, but it was unexpected and almost out of place. In any case, it's good writing (despite occasional flashes of vulgarity that is not atypical of Scalzi), and I appreciated the way Redshirts ended, as much for what it said about writing as it said about Star Trek.

Doing two a day workouts is an inconvenience for some.<br /><br />Lastly, what is wrong with doing low intensity cardio on a cut or bulk? HIIT is a great workout, but maybe more so for people that are trying to lose weight, butdepending on the person's activity level, it may do more harm than good because it is a grueling workout. 20-30 minutes of that is overkill and nobody would be able to do 20 30-60 minutes sprints for 30 minutes. B000XUBFE2|5.0|I'll be honest and say that I wasn't all that excited about The Book Thief. While I feel like YA fiction is kind of in a great period right now, there's still a serious signal-to-noise deficiency out there (as there is in every genre, to be fair), and knowing that the book touched on the Holocaust didn't fill me with hope. (This is not meant as a slight on the Holocaust so much as a comment on the fact that it seems like a staple for bad writers hoping to wring out an emotional narrative that wouldn't otherwise exist; moreover, when books like Night exist, how much more can you truly say that hasn't been said more painfully and hauntingly?) And yet, I found myself absolutely wrapped up in The Book Thief, and much of that has to do with the voice of the narrator. Rather than allowing the titular thief - a young orphan being raised in a small German town as the second World War breaks out - to narrate her own tale, Zusak decides to give the telling of this tale to no less a personage than Death. It's a gutsy choice, but a brilliant one, as it gives the book a far more universal and haunting feel than it might have had otherwise. Let a gutsy orphan tell the tale, and the book could easily slide into sentimentality, or be just a simple coming of age story set against the backdrop of the war; let Death narrate, and the tale becomes far more sobering, as the effects of the war can never be forgotten, no matter how peaceful life seems in the small German town. The story mixes a lot of elements - a love of books and words that leads to thievery, a Jewish refugee who's made a name as a brawler, a young boy obsessed with Jesse Owens - and at times, the book feels dangerously close to slipping into chaos or absurdity. But somehow, it never does; even the more absurd elements feel loaded with portent and danger, a nice side effect of Zusak's writing and scope that always reminds the reader of the dangers to come. While The Book Thief is definitely a book about World War II (and to some degree, the Holocaust, though it's treated as part of the overall devastation and death dealt out by the war), in many ways, it's more of a story about words and their impact, and the way that simple gestures can mean everything, especially when they seem to do nothing at all. It's a book I was wary of, but I can't deny that I was deeply impressed by it; at its best moments, The Book Thief offers up something truly powerful and unforgettable, and any chance to expose a young audience to such things shouldn't be shrugged off.to some degree, the Holocaust, though it's treated as part of the overall devastation and death dealt out by the war), in many ways, it's more of a story about words and their impact, and the way that simple gestures can mean everything, especially when they seem to do nothing at all. It's a book I was wary of, but I can't deny that I was deeply impressed by it; at its best moments, The Book Thief offers up something truly powerful and unforgettable, and any chance to expose a young audience to such things shouldn't be shrugged off.

B006LSZECO|5.0|I picked up Gone Girl based on an odd coincidence - two unrelated people on my Twitter feed both raving about it within hours of each other - and it's a coincidence that I'm deeply glad for, because otherwise I might have missed out on this twisted, surprisingly, gleefully dark thriller. Gone Girl starts off simply enough: it's Nick Dunne's fifth wedding anniversary, but his wife Amy has disappeared. There are signs of a struggle in his living room, but otherwise - nothing. In alternating chapters, we see Nick struggling to find out the truth and Amy's diary detailing the idyllic early days of their relationship...but cracks start to show, and soon. And as the media starts to turn on Nick, we start to question whether Nick is as good as we think he is...but it's not long before Flynn starts to pull the rug out from under the reader again and again, until we're forced to question just about every single aspect of the book we've read so far. This is my first exposure to Gillian Flynn, but it won't be the last; even if we were only talking about the plot, I'd have to rank Gone Girl as one of the most involving and gripping thrillers I've read in years - I absolutely could not put it down, and found myself reading it every time I sat even for a moment. But more than that, there's Flynn's great writing, which allows her to create both Nick and Amy as complex characters based on nothing more than their own narration and discussion - characters that can still surprise you greatly in ways that I wouldn't dream of revealing. Gone Girl plays out brilliantly, all the way to an ending that seems to have really upset some people, and that baffles me; to me, the ending is a brilliantly dark punchline to the novel, a pitch-black immersion into the darkness that's prevalent in every page of the book anyway. (In fact, for my money, the ending elevates it from a good thriller to a brilliant one.) I've come across one person already comparing Flynn to the legendary Patricia Highsmith, and it's a great comparison; both seem to have a knack for understanding how people work, both have created absolutely legendary sociopathic villains (and, oh, is there ever a stunning villain lurking somewhere in Gone Girl), and both were willing to follow their characters to the absolute heart of darkness if it felt right. Consider me a fan already - I plan on reading Flynn's two earlier books as soon as I possibly can, but even if they don't live up to Gone Girl, I've still gotten one absolutely astonishing thriller out of this experience.and it's a great comparison; both seem to have a knack for understanding how people work, both have created absolutely legendary sociopathic villains (and, oh, is there ever a stunning villain lurking somewhere in Gone Girl), and both were willing to follow their characters to the absolute heart of darkness if it felt right. 1613821395|5.0|A pure novel from the 19th century, right in the middle of Dickens' era and yet so much more dynamic than Dickens' stories that are systematically slowed down by long descriptions that bring nothing to the plot or the story itself. Here none of those boring descriptive digressions.<br /><br />The story is a long story but the novel sticks to the story and never gets astray. It is told from the point of view of various characters, which gives to the story telling a variety it might not have had otherwise. That diversity enables us to go through that long story without getting lost in attention or translation. In other words it is tremendously modern.<br /><br />The story cannot be told really without deflowering it, so I will only make one comment on it. It is a story about women and the slow change that occurred in the middle of the 19th century under Queen Victoria. This feminine touch, this feminist touch can be seen at various levels.<br /><br />First it clearly shows the social segregation that touches women: women of the higher or upper class (no matter how little that higher or upper class may be) cannot in any way love or marry under their position. It is a question D.H. Lawrence will illustrate very well in his \\"Lady Chatterley's Lover\\". But here there is a slight evolution towards an exception. And the higher or upper class is shown as particularly anti-women even to the point of being over-courteous or over-attentionate with them. Too much sugar ruins the pie, obviously.<br /><br />Second it clearly shows how swift and intelligent a woman can be when she is kept by nature in the position of not being a belle, a desirable woman. In fact the book pushes the point a little too far with providing that woman with a male face on a female body. She will of course not marry. Why should she since she is more than plain. But she is intelligent, resistant, resilient and she has many other qualities that are hinted at as being masculine, though it is not always a hint. If the woman is beautiful, and there are two in the novel, either she will be a merchandise to be traded in a monetary marriage, or she will be classified dangerous, lunatic and she will be locked up in an Asylum.<br /><br />On the other hand a handsome man from the higher or upper class can seduce all the women he wants, and since he is limited in his advances with women from his own class, he finds no limits with the lower and working classes. That's what can be called fair equality for the sexes. If such a woman from the lower or working classes finds herself pregnant, she better finds a husband that will cover up the disreputable mistake, which is though no mistake of the woman at all but the exploitation of tat woman by some aristocrat.<br /><br />Third when a woman is victimized by her husband she has no way to appeal, to defend herself, to ask for help from lawyers and justice. She has to submit, be obedient and patient. That is so powerfully expressed that it becomes obsessional and such a victimized woman can only be helped by other women. One exception: one man who helps a \\"lunatic\\" woman to escape and an upper class woman to love.<br /><br />This book is thus a perfect depicting of the Victorian society of the 19th century at that tender moment when it starts moving on essential questions, particularly that of women.<br /><br />What's more then the book is a thriller in a way, the story of a crime and the solving of the case with a lot of suspense. It reminds us of the Bronte sisters and their dark novels. In one last word then it is a novel written by a woman and it fairly represents the important feminine literature of the 19th century, a feminine literature that is often eclipsed by Dickens.<br /><br />Dr Jacques COULARDEAU. If the woman is beautiful, and there are two in the novel, either she will be a merchandise to be traded in a monetary marriage, or she will be classified dangerous, lunatic and she will be locked up in an Asylum.<br /><br />On the other hand a handsome man from the higher or upper class can seduce all the women he wants, and since he is limited in his advances with women from his own class, he finds no limits with the lower and working classes. We encounter something clearly fictional, that somehow seems real. I've never found a checkmark on the bottom of a page so terrifying.<br /><br />The book is experimental fiction at its most perfectly executed. Even the \\"editor\\" of the book is a narrative character. 0525478817|5.0|Hazel is a teenager diagnosed with terminal cancer. She goes to a cancer support group where she meets a boy named Augustus Waters who will change her forever. Throughout the book, the two grow with each other and find a love both profound and exquisite, even in the shadow of death.<br /><br />You know those stories that leave you numb? The ones that you finish, and then set aside and stare at blank space for a while so that you can process what you had just finished reading? Yeah, this was that kind of book. And the thing was, I was completely blindsided by how great this book was. Okay, so The Fault in Our Stars is a cancer book. Apparently this is a niche genre, because after I finished this book, I read some other reviews that mentioned other \\"cancer books\\" like this one. I would assume that a cancer book had a prerequisite amount of melancholy and suffering, and I typically shy away from those kinds of books. Maybe that's why I found Stars so enchanting, as opposed to some people who disliked it. I'm not familiar with cancer books, so this was new territory for me.<br /><br />Something about Hazel's wry, snappy narration is charming and completely engrossing, and kept me reading long into the night. Hazel does not dwell on her illness. It's a constant, and it's been a part of her life since she was a child, but her sense of humor shines through, and makes the subject of terminal cancer an easier pill to swallow for the reader. I truly did like Hazel as a character. She's self-deprecating and mature for her age, which instantly made me identify with her. I particularly appreciated her relationship with her parents, and how she seems to take on the adult role when it comes to her illness. Green's prose sparkles with life, and he wrote the voice of Hazel so well that I was initially surprised that the book was written by a man, and not a woman.<br /><br />Hazel and her love interest, Augustus, had a unique and interesting relationship, even if I found them a bit contrived. This was the only part in the book that I took issue with. I found that Hazel and Augustus were unusually well-spoken for kids their age. Then again, I was the same at that age, so who am I to play skeptical reader? However, I think that they acted unrealistically old and adult, when they're supposed to be seventeen. That took me out of the story a bit, because even though they looked and seemed like teens, Hazel and Augustus had the dynamic of two adults. I think that Mr. Green needs to learn how to truly emulate the voice of a young person, rather than using them as a mouthpiece for his own thoughts.<br /><br />I found the love story a bit forced. A teen cancer patient (who strongly resembles Natalie Portman &agrave; la V for Vendetta, as is repeatedly mentioned throughout the book, as if the reader will forget how attractive Hazel is even when she's sick and fighting cancer) happens to run into an equally, if not more so, gorgeous boy who is in remission and is decidedly articulate for a teenage boy... it all felt a little too neatly set up to be truly believable. However, as their relationship evolved and I got to see a more three-dimensional Augustus, I really started to like the two of them together and ultimately fell in love with their love.<br /><br />I found myself completely enchanted by the plot, in which Hazel, with the help of Augustus, seeks to speak to the author of a book that changed her life. By book's end, I found myself really caring about the characters, and despite knowing what I signed myself up for, the ending of the book was an emotional sucker punch to the gut. I knew with this kind of book that something was going to happen, but that didn't make it any easier to handle. The Fault in Our Stars left me emotionally drained, but uplifted. I can see why Green has such a cult following online... his writing is full of heart and soul.ontrived. This was the only part in the book that I took issue with. 030758836X|5.0|Wow. I didn't intend to read Gillian Flynn's Gone Girl when I did. Two other books sat on the top of the reading pile, begging for a thorough read, the Amazon reader review thing, and so on. But one dark day, I needed a book with an edge to it, and having peeked at a sample page of Gone Girl already, I knew where to find that edge.<br /><br />Gone Girl is the story of Nick and Amy, a seemingly perfect couple to anyone looking in from the outside. But at the start of the book, on the couple's fifth anniversary, when Nick comes home to a ransacked living room and a missing wife, the marriage has already deteriorated to a far from perfect state. Talk about your love hate relationship. Nick and Amy have one in spades at this point - mostly hate, but with love so excruciatingly painted around the edges you can't imagine a better combination.<br /><br />Nick's clumsy handling of himself during the police investigation and an accumulating, increasingly-damning pile of evidence soon point the finger at him as the murderer. We, the readers, don't know whether he is one or not, even though we've been reading most passages from his first person point of view.<br /><br />So is he or isn't he? I won't spoil it. Let me instead touch on two compelling aspects of this novel. First, Gillian's voice is one that many writers would kill for (bad pun, I know). Through the first half of the book she alternates her passages from Nick's point of view (written in a Jess Walter voice, and you never think for one second that hmmm this is a woman trying to write from a man's POV), and Amy's point of view as found in diary entries written before the climactic murder or abduction or whatever actually happened (because I still refuse to tell). Amy's voice is much different than Nick's and I love that. She's brilliant and has a fascinating perceptiveness about each of those many people who just can't help but revolve around her irresistible gravitational force.<br /><br />The other can't-put-it-down aspect of this novel is the quick pacing both before and after a sudden switch in plot halfway through. By that point, as a reader, you think you know the whole story and how it will all end, and you can't imagine what the second half of the book will be for.  Why isn't this a novella? What's coming next?<br /><br />What's coming next is motive, and true circumstances, plans going awry, plans being reshaped, and a remarkable elevation of compromise to an art form.<br /><br />I loved this book.<br /><br />Joe

0553103547|4.0|An enthralling, epic tale set in a place reminiscent of medieval Europe, Britain, and beyond.  Many different storylines are interwoven to create a fascinating and complete world filled with treachery, deceit, and intrigue.  There is also love and honor, particularly in the person of Lord Eddard Stark, who is called by his land's king, Robert Baratheon, to leave his home behind and act as the King's Hand (in other words, his right-hand man).  He tries valiantly to serve the king, while maintaining his integrity.  This proves to be nigh impossible, since every man and woman has an agenda, and he knows next to no-one, save the king himself.<br /><br />Cersei, the queen, is from the rich and powerful House of Lannister, and she has her own plans for the running of the kingdom.  She loves not the king, but is quite fond of her brother, Jamie, who stays quite snugly by her side.  She is grooming her young and spoiled son, Joffrey, to take the throne should some misfortune befall her drunken lout of a husband.  As soon as the boy enters the scene, it is easy to see that he would not be a kinder, gentler ruler. 'Tis best to hope for a long and hearty life for the current lord of the realm.<br /><br />The twin brother to Jamie Lannister, Tyrion is my favorite character by far.  Called the Imp, due to his dwarfism, Tyrion is at the bottom of the food chain amongst the illustrious Lannister clan.  While others in his family are tall, fair and golden, including his twin brother, Tyrion is an embarrassment to his father.  He doesn't \\"measure up\\", if you will.  His father cannot see the sharp wits and keen judge of character that are gifted to Tyrion.  He only sees his son's lack of height, and takes that to mean that Tyrion can be no leader of men, and thus, of no worth to the Lannisters. (He doesn't know what he's missing, as Tyrion is a pure delight, and every page he is on is to be savored.)<br /><br />In exile are the remains of the Great House Targaryen, brother and sister, Viserys and Daenerys.  The Targaryens are the House of the Dragons, quite literally.  At one time a great and powerful dynasty, with dragons at their command, now the dragons are gone, and the kingdom has been lost (to the king mentioned above, no less).  In desperation to win back his late father's throne, cold-blooded Viserys sells his sister into wedlock with Khal Drogo, the leader of the barbaric tribe, the Dothraki.  A nomadic people,  the Dothraki treasure their horses, and have such a wealth of gold that they wear the metal in their hair and adorning their bodies.  These people could be a distinct threat to the kingdom now run by the Baratheons and Lannisters, if they did not hold a fear of crossing the sea that separates one land from the other.  Viserys sees this, and tries to force an alliance with the Dothraki.  Unfortunately for Viserys, it is clear that he is weak. His sense of superiority over the Dothraki, (and everyone else), does not help his cause, either.  Things look bleak for the House Tagaryen.<br /><br />There are many more characters in this tale, and all are important pawns in the chess match that is The Game of Thrones. B0070Y46UY|3.0|For those of you who have already read the book, you'll note the provocative nature of my title. For those of you who haven't, you're probably aware there is a tiger in the boat with our protagonist. Richard Parker is the tiger (or perhaps more as you'll come to discover).<br /><br />As such, we all have a Richard Parker, an entity of such profound existential proportions and life-changing consequences that we have to deal with him, tame him, maybe eliminate him altogether. Mine's God. Thus, I avoided this book for years, mostly for theological reasons. My God-fearing friends recommended it time and again, but I had to be patient, wait to discover it for myself. Finally, I put it on a holiday shopping list and found it under the tree. I read the first 100 pages or so, then took a break for about six months before finishing in just a couple days. I can't explain why I left Pi stranded there with his tiger for so long, perhaps I was testing him, or he was testing me.<br /><br />As a story, Life of Pi is intriguing and curious. Readers can put themselves in Pi's place, more for the survival aspect than the more fantastical interactions with the zoo-inspired food chain. As someone interested in science and story, I was captivated by the descriptions of desalinating water, fishing for turtles, and otherwise traversing the scope of the human capability for continued existence.<br /><br />Throughout the work, we're treated to glimpses of theme, revelations of everything from faith to coping mechanisms. Regardless of other reviewers' treatments of the piece, I find Martel might even be suggesting faith is itself a defense mechanism. Critics have equated the novel to God and all things theological. I see it as a twisted horror story dressed in allegory; I hope both aspects are handled with due care in the upcoming film version. God and faith are mine, entities I've explored, researched, doubted, and more. For this reason, I identified with the novel in terms of those using faith as a defense mechanism, a reality made evident just by simply watching the news or talking to people.<br /><br />Life of Pi is worth reading, worth discussing, and worth each reader developing his or her opinions. 145210655X|4.0|Normally I save my reviewing for longer books but this book was so much fun I just had to make a plug to help get the word out.  Darth Vader and Son was recommended a couple of months ago when I was reading another \\"Dad\\" book (\\"Dad Rules.\\").<br /><br />Darth Vader and Son is a cute and fun ~60 page art/comic book.  While the book doesn't have a particular plot arc that it follows, it has great storytelling moments that are portrayed fabulously.  As the title implies, this book is about the interactions of Darth Vader and his son.  (For the 1% of you who aren't up on Star Wars lore...be aware that this book poses some pretty blatant spoilers, though I think the spoiler grace period there ended in the early 80s).<br /><br />In the book, Luke Skywalker is a young (5-8 year old?) boy wandering the universe with his dad...the intimidating Darth Vader.  The scenes range from endearingly sweet to laugh out loud hilarious.  Aside from the blatant relation to the Star Wars universe, there are a bunch of more subtle nods to lesser known elements.<br /><br />The art style is solid and a lot of fun.  The author (Jeffrey Brown) has a website where you can get a glimpse of his art style and find links to his other work.  I haven't read or seen anything else by him but my whole family has enjoyed reading through Darth Vader and Son.  Even though we've all read it, my kids love holding the book in front of another family member with a \\"Lookit! Lookit!\\" to get us giggling again at the fun juxtaposition of the powerful Darth Vader and a cute little Luke.<br /><br />This is a fun little read for any Star Wars fan who is willing to poke a little fun at the universe.  Great for parents, kids or just kids at heart.<br /><br />****<br />4 out of 5 stars

That's the truth of the story- excelling, winning, overcoming, doesn't mean you don't lose pieces of yourself along the way, or that you reach old age and success in happiness and health.  What keeps Enders going is hope that at some point you will be at peace with yourself, your performance, your environment, your role, others' roles, relationships, well- everything.

B007NUP6GS|5.0|Bach's six (or so) surviving motets rank very high with those who love his vocal music. This is probably because they afford direct, continuous contact with SINGERS doing what Bach was best at: COUNTERPOINT. To put it more broadly, Bach was supremely gifted at creating complex but appealing multi-voiced musical fabrics guided by harmony. Just as we prize works like Art of Fugue, The \\"Goldberg\\" Variations, and The Well-Tempered Clavier because of their great fugues and other contrapuntal inventions, so can we enjoy the motets. Vocally, they are extremely rich per-square-inch in Bachian counterpoint.<br /><br />Besides counterpoint, the motets have expressive, varied texts, usually taken from hymns or scripture; thus in a literary sense they also stand head and shoulders above much of the third-rate poetic dreck with which Bach was saddled -- it must be said -- in the cantatas, for example.<br /><br />An earlier recording of the motets by this conductor with the Monteverdi Choir and English Baroque Soloists ([[ASIN:B000005E9X Bach: Motets BWV 225-231, Cantatas]]) also won critical praise. I have the LP, and it's glorious, especially for its varied instrumental accompaniments and its glistening analog sound. This new edition dispenses with the instruments except for a basic continuo group. That has the beneficial effect of focusing attention on the vocal ensemble, which remains small -- perhaps three or four to a part for works that vary from four to five to eight parts.<br /><br />Gardiner's long experience with Bach, and his use of seasoned vocalists, pays off. Every measure glows. Every phrase is alive with rhythmic energy. One of the great delights of this new recording is that you can hear the singers exuberantly doing things in the excitement of live performance that might not have withstood the patch-and-edit process in a studio recording. (Note: I am NOT saying that the performances are ever sloppy or over-the-top. Absolutely not. They're just happier, sadder, more rhythmic, etc. etc. than you might have thought Bach could get.)<br /><br />Check out the cover of the CD (actually a nice little book, with Gardiner's insightful notes on each work plus texts and translations). It features high-wire artist Philippe Petit inching his way across some chasm or another, barefoot. Anyone who's ever tried to perform this music will understand why Gardiner and company chose it.<br /><br />Top recommendation, without reservations.more rhythmic, etc. etc. than you might have thought Bach could get.)<br /><br />Check out the cover of the CD (actually a nice little book, with Gardiner's insightful notes on each work plus texts and translations). It features high-wire artist Philippe Petit inching his way across some chasm or another, barefoot. While the musicianship isn't as aggressive as it could have been, I will say I do like Kirk's solo around the 4:30 mark. Next we have the title track at almost 10 minutes long this track thunders the whole way through, once it gets going that is. Plus this is Metallica's most politically charged moment in their career, which I find pretty cool. Very few genres of music can actually cover topics as big as say political corruption, child abuse, and being a vegetable that had his arms and legs blown off by a landmine. But it is a decent ballad and adds to the darkness of the album. And last \\"Dyers Eve\\" My third favorite Metallica song of all time. A great all out thrasher. 0345403959|5.0|Belgarath the Sorcerer is best read after both the Belgariad and the Mallorean. Although it is a standalone novel, the prologue is built upon events that happen at the end of the Mallorean.<br /><br />How to describe the novel? Calling it a history book is a grave injustice, on the scale of calling a Lamborghini a car (which it is, of course, but surely you can come up with far more dazzling ways to describe a Lamborghini.) \\"Belgarath the Sorcerer\\" is the story of one man's love--for his god, for his wife, for his \\"brothers\\", for his daughters, and for people. It is the story of duty and responsibility (and a sense of humor) that persists in spite of decade-long diversions, centuries-long sidetracks, and millennia-long grief.<br /><br />Eddings manages pace very well--speeding through centuries in a paragraph, and then spending whole chapters on the events of a few weeks--without leaving the reader dizzy from wondering what just happened. The flow of the story seamlessly carries the reader along through detailed scenes. Characters are both vivid and memorable, and readers will enjoy seeing how all those insider jokes from the Belgariad started.<br /><br />A thoroughly enjoyable read. When you're done with this, pick up his next book, [[ASIN:0345422554 Polgara the Sorceress (Malloreon)]]

Next is Escape and it is their most underrated song. It retains a New Wave of Heavy Metal feeling to it, but it gets the Metallica overhaul. And the lyrics are inspiring in a way. Now for the most intense track on the album, Creeping Death. At over six minutes, this song will DESTROY your neck. It talks about how the angel of death freed the Hebrew people from the Egyptians as in the book of Exodus in the Bible. Absolutely one of the best tracks. And last is the instrumental The Call of Ktulu. Dave Mustaine has credits on this song as well and it just proves how great both bands Metallica and Megadeth are. One of my favorite instrumentals and has a Rust In Peace feeling to it. Overall, Metallica's second effort put them in the lead of other metal bands around this time, only thing comparable would be Slayer's release. Metallica released an amazing masterpiece of an album that stands the test of time, and the test of other bands today. I think it had something to do with my love and respect for the name on the cover, who most certainly was not the author of this adolescent clunker.<br /><br />Everything that is great about Michael Crichton is missing from this gangly, awkward, lowbrow wreckage of what was probably a scribble of an idea from the great author. Most notably, is the science. Crichton is known for divining a carefully crafted premise from real-life cutting edge research and adding just enough fictional technology to make the concept fly. He would painstakingly work within the bounds of his tweaked reality to keep the story exciting and plausible at the same time. Conversely, Mr. Preston can't even seem to keep spatial relationships consistent in his micro-world, and his source material appears to cut off somewhere around Mrs. Carmichael's seventh grade Life Sciences class. Yet, I understand that Crichton's talents were unique in that sense, and I could forgive a shortcoming in this area. It is Preston's mangling of basic literary craft that angered me most.<br /><br />The storyline is contrived to the point that I would place it just below no-name juvenile fiction one might find in the clearance bin at the dollar store. The characters have the depth and dimension of a middle school lit project. I have to wonder how this book even made it to press in this state. The basic style and structure of the writing does not, in my opinion, read like the work of a professional.<br /><br />I love everything Michael Crichton has written, so I can only imagine that his involvement with this manuscript extended no further than the partially completed forward and perhaps a few scribbles in a notebook. This is the opposite of vintage Crichton and it makes me furious that his name is on the cover.

I went for Sector 2 first as they were the key Rush albums in the band's catalog and was blown away at how much better they sounded to the previous remasters, each album was improved upon in some way or another.<br />Then in April of 2012, I acquired Sectors 1 and 3. This review is for the third Sector box set.<br />The albums in this third of three Rush box sets are the band's last five albums for Mercury/PolyGram (the 1982 masterpiece Signals, 1984's Grace Under Pressure, 1985's Power Windows, 1987's Hold Your Fire and the early 1989 double live A Show of Hands). What's cool about this boxed set is that all of the albums are packaged in miniature vinyl sleeves so it's just like buying the original vinyl albums all over again.<br />I don't need to rehash the reviews of these five albums again (if you want my opinion read the reviews of each one) but what I can say is that the sonic quality on this box set is incredible. Unlike the first two box sets, this box set was not defective though there are alternate mixes of some of the Hold Your Fire tracks (\\"Time Stand Still\\" has extra drums, \\"Mission\\" has some strings I never heard, extra chimes on \\"Tai Shan\\" and a few other surprises).<br />The album remixed in 5.1 on this box set is 1982's Signals. On \\"Subdivisions\\", \\"Analog Man\\" and (heck) the whole album sounds great and I heard parts not on the original 1982 mix which has been seemingly forever in my brain since 1990 so I heard some new vocal deliveries and instrument parts which were not on the original mix due to the constraints of vinyl. \\"The Weapon\\" actually has some extra bits of music not on the original stereo mix plus \\"Losing It\\" has a few seconds extra on playout not on original mix.<br />Also, the packaging is incredible with the miniature vinyl sleeves plus a booklet with great pictures and album credits and also each disc's box looks like part of a roadcase and the spine of Sector 2 has a 1982 era picture of Neil Peart.<br />Sector 3 (like the two Sector box sets aside this) by Rush is a must own and if you want to replace your 1997 remasters and/or the MFSL I say go for it.<br />RECOMMENDED!lbum sounds great and I heard parts not on the original 1982 mix which has been seemingly forever in my brain since 1990 so I heard some new vocal deliveries and instrument parts which were not on the original mix due to the constraints of vinyl. \\"The Weapon\\" actually has some extra bits of music not on the original stereo mix plus \\"Losing It\\" has a few seconds extra on playout not on original mix.<br />Also, the packaging is incredible with the miniature vinyl sleeves plus a booklet with great pictures and album credits and also each disc's box looks like part of a roadcase and the spine of Sector 2 has a 1982 era picture of Neil Peart.<br />Sector 3 (like the two Sector box sets aside this) by Rush is a must own and if you want to replace your 1997 remasters and/or the MFSL I say go for it.<br />RECOMMENDED!
0156030306|5.0|The award winning (Hugo and Nebuola Award) novel Flowers for Algernon by Daniel Keyes is about a lonely mentally handicapped man named Charlie Gordon who works in a bakery as a janitor. The book follows him through his life during an operation and his adventures after. During his journey he meets Algernon the only individual he can identify with because Algernon had the same operation.<br /><br />The book is written in a series of reports documenting the day to day life of Charlie, which makes it very manageable and compelling to read even if you only have a few minutes. This style of writing also allows you to empathize with Charlie throughout the whole story due to the fact you have insight into what he is feeling and experiencing. The book deals with the themes of loneliness, finding out who you are, where you came from, and behavior. If you have any interest in what it means to be human; you will enjoy this book.<br /><br />If you are looking for a science fiction novel with flashy characters and futuristic environments, this is not the book you are looking for. It is about the human condition and about the struggles people face.<br /><br />There are some sparsely placed religious conversations and scenes of a sexual nature that may offend some people.<br /><br />I thought Flowers for Algernon was an excellent read because it had many life lessons. The quotation, \\"A simple idea. Trust myself,\\" is very important to anyone, having faith in what you are doing is vital for success. The report style of writing makes it very easy to read and I would often find myself saying just one more report, then I will do something else.<br /><br />The book leaves you with many questions: Does intelligence hinder happiness? Would Charlie have been better off without the operation? What will the rest of his life be like?<br /><br />Although this book is fifty years old it is still perfectly valid. This resistance to the passage of time makes it a true classic in my opinion. I would recommend this book to anyone and if you read it before as a teenager, perhaps it is time you revisit it.

He truly is an amazing author, descriptive and precise, capable of creating mood and atmosphere that makes you feel as though you are a member of the family throughout the entire story. Steinbeck is not only socially and politically observant of the times; he also adds depth to his story by depicting the conditions that the people lived in, and describing the desires of not just individuals, but the nation during the struggle of the great depression, after the dust bowl. The story is an epic tale of one Oklahoman family and their forced migration west.  After having their farm and home foreclosed on by the bank, the Joads are forced to leave the only home they have ever known.  With word that there is work out west they are forced to make their trek across the country to survive.  Even though it happened almost eighty years ago, The Grapes of Wrath is reminiscent of what is happening now in politics.  With such a large gap between the wealthy and the poor there is a sense that all people want is a fair chance in this book. I would recommend this The Grapes of Wrath to anyone who has interests in politics, history, or social sciences.  John Steinbeck has a very good understanding of these and even makes a few suggestions on ideas that he thought were good and later came to be( Like rotating crops in farming, the formation of unions, and other regulations that were put into place after the great depression). John Steinbeck demanded that a full copy of \\"The Battle Hymn of the Republic,\\" be put in the first edition of his book. It is a book about second chances, patriotism, love that never gave up, and--most of all--faith. Adam Brown's extraordinary life was an incredible--albeit difficult--journey from utter hopelessness to a life that was filled with purpose.<br /><br />Right from the start this book pulls you in and makes it very hard to have to put it down to attend to other things. I remember laughing out loud at one point in the beginning of the book when the author, Eric Blehm, was illustrating what Brown had been like as a toddler--the similarity to my own son and the understanding of how his mother must have felt each time he did something outlandish gave me some common ground with the story and drew me deeper into it's pages. Brown's never-quit attitude (despite seemingly impossible odds on more than one occasion) made me examine my own attitudes when faced with not-anywhere-near-as-impossible of odds.<br /><br />I don't want to give anything away, but the title of the book truly sum's up Brown's life in a single word--from the start of his life to his final breath--he truly was fearless. Chapter by chapter, you meet characters for whom the heat of war has further fractured the cracks and fault lines in their souls - from young men who go crazy trying to keep themselves sane, to opportunists who take advantage of the most ridiculous circumstances, to generals and colonels who play power games simply to indulge in their own power.<br /><br />What Heller's book shows us, when we laugh at the characters and their actions, is that we are not so different from this lunatic cast - that our own offices, churches, and homes are filled with the same characters and the same flaws.  Some of those characters and flaws are funny, and some are downright frightening - but none of them is without critique.  We are all cracked and we will all shatter - the only hope is escape.<br /><br />Heller's book is unapologetically escapist.  When you see the world as an absurd series of events, there is little else to do but escape.  If you become a part of that landscape, you lose yourself.  Yossarian, the protagonist, refuses to let himself completely melt into the canvas of the scene that is painted around him and seeks a way out. In the life of a Christian, this way out is Christ - the escape for sinners who would rather not go on sinning, even though they have already done so and have broken themselves and others in that process.  Heller does not paint Yossarian's escape so clearly, but does give us hope that Yossarian will escape.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book for the most part. This has the best narative flow, perhaps, but there is a little more analysis in the other works, and certainly this is less unhappy than Hastings brutal retelling of the events of 1939-1945.<br /><br />What Beevor does better than those other two is explore the war in China: perhaps he even does this better than Hastings \\"Retribution\\" about the Pacific War. Of course, that means you ignore a lot of suffering by the Chinese peasantry, which you cannot overlook in today's world, which is still largely shaped by WWII.<br /><br />In short, every theatre of war is covered in some degree of detail, together with how they all interrelated, and also with the machinations of Stalin, Roosevelt, Churchill, and Hitler (and to a degree, de Gaulle). B004LWZW6U|3.0|The recent remake of [[ASIN:B002ZG98L4 Piranha]] was nearly pitch-perfect. Gleefully hapless characters (portrayed with excellent B-movie abandon by the likes of Elisabeth Shue, Christopher Lloyd, and even Richard Dreyfus) trotted through the cliche-riddled story and provided both laughs and inventive, over-the-top gore. It wasn't out to win any awards, but what it WAS out to do -- amuse and disturb -- it did quite well. So, of course, they had to make a sequel.<br /><br />Unfortunately, they neglected to include half of the original formula. Truly excessive T&A? Check. Obvious character archetypes doing/saying obvious things? Check. Humor, either hokey, offensive, or smart? Check. Scares and grind-em-up deaths?<br /><br />Not so much.<br /><br />The problem here lies right in the set-up: an unapologetically awful man (played by David Koechner) opens a water park (with an adults only section) and contrives to fill it for free from a nearby lake. Three guesses how that plays out.<br /><br />Yes. The piranhas infest the water park, and therein lies the problem. I don't know if you've ever been to a water park, but every one I've ever visited appeared to have been planned and designed by a blind man with permanent vertigo. This is usually in keeping with the looping, curving, twisting paths of the various water slides and lazy rivers, and it means it's not that hard to get turned around if the park is big. The point being: filming in one must be hell.<br /><br />There is never any real sense of place to the film, and the park is never given a wide enough shot to really show the scale of the disaster. At the same time, the very fact that people are inches away from simply hopping out of the water reduces both the scale, spectacle, and believability of any of the chaos. There's a ten or fifteen minute scene of people thrashing about in water that is two feet wide and three feet deep. Gone is even any attempt at terror or (failing that) anything close to the stomach-churning destruction found in the first film.<br /><br />The movie tries to make up for this in other ways. There are a couple of piranha attacks outside of the park, one of which is so over-the-top ludicrous that it is almost annoying. I won't give away any details, but I will say that it's one step away from characters shooting live piranhas out of shotguns like some kind of Itchy and Scratchy cartoon. But that's probably the point. While the first film was an aggressive and ham-fisted satire of Swarm Horror, this movie seeks only to be a cartoon of it. It's not really the same thing, and so this film -- if you'll pardon the pun -- very much lacks the bite that made the first one such a success.<br /><br />As a live action cartoon, though, it's not bad. David Hasselhoff does a great job of making fun of himself AND the film. (\\"Stupid ginger boy,\\" he complains when he hears the screams of a kid who's getting eaten. \\"Why did you get back in the water?\\") He's one of the better parts of the film, although he is also overused. Ving Rhames, Paul Scheer, and Christopher Lloyd, on the other hand, are woefully underused. They brighten up their brief blips in the film, but all of them feel like they were added to the script at the last minute. There are some puns and gags that work, and some that don't.<br /><br />It feels half done, is the point, and this is even evident in the run time. IMDB will tell you it's 83 minutes long, but that's counting the very slow credits crawl at the end. Perhaps knowing the deficit in their film, the makers put outtakes, deleted scenes, and a gag reel over half of these end credits. It's kind of like a nice, advanced special features addition, and I enjoyed it for what it was, but I also saw it for what it was meant to be: a quick, easy distraction from the fact that the film was more entertaining behind the scenes than in front of it.<br /><br />Fun for what it is, but not as fun as it wants to be or should live up to. You might enjoy yourselfif you have low expectations, or you might just have more fun watching Skinemax and putting some fish in a blender.if you have low expectations, or you might just have more fun watching Skinemax and putting some fish in a blender.

B005LAIHYU|3.0|I guess we owe Abraham Lincoln twin salvos of thanks, for ridding the nation of slavery... and for killing vampires. ABRAHAM LINCOLN, VAMPIRE HUNTER, I think, would've hugely benefited from a more tongue-in-cheek approach. I mean, there's already a built-in sense of the ridiculous just from the notion of our illustrious 16th President chopping down entire bevies of vampires with his trusty axe. But, for whatever reason, this film adaptation of Seth Grahame-Smith's hit mash-up novel opts for a more somber presentation. Grahame-Smith himself wrote the screenplay so you can't even rant that this isn't the author's vision. This was one of the summer flicks I was looking forward to. Yikes.<br /><br />Honest Abe? No, not quite. There were dark secrets Abraham Lincoln firmly kept under his signature stove top hat. In this wild alternate biography, the narrative posits that not only was Abraham Lincoln the Great Emancipator, he also saved humanity from a relentless vampire incursion. To quote Lincoln's voiceover narration: \\"History prefers legends to men... However history remembers me before I was a President, it shall only remember a fraction of the truth...\\" The film opens in Pigeon Creek, Indiana, in the year 1818, when Abraham was a young lad, as we learn the horrifying origins of his lifelong obsession with hunting and destroying vampires. From there the film skips ahead to Abe as a young man (now played by Benjamin Walker) as he miserably fails in his first stab at vampire slaying. Enter Henry Sturgess (Dominic Cooper), enigmatic vampire expert who takes the inexperienced Abe under his wing and trains him in the art of tracking and snuffing out them malevolent creatures of the night. Cue the ridiculous(ly fun) training montage and the edge of Abe's favorite axe getting treated with silver, never mind that silver is notoriously the werewolf's bane, not the vampire's.<br /><br />Abe should've known better than to mix his politics with his vampire slaying. But fate has tapped Abe for bigger and better things. I know we stepped into the theater mostly to eyeball Abe taking out vampires, but, for the sake of some verisimilitude and as grounding support, I wish that the film had been more eloquent in depicting this most iconic of figures. I wish it had gone more into exhibiting Lincoln's statesmanship, that his orations were delivered with more conviction, that Benjamin Walker had fleshed out his role more convincingly and with more weight and charisma. The film pays lip service to the historical moments in Lincoln's life. Really, these moments only serve as framing points for the CG-heavy action sequences and to occasionally remind the audience that, hey, in case you forgot, this is the freakin' 16th President. Look, there's the Gettysburg address.<br /><br />I love, love, love those sequences in which Abe was dropping the mighty axe on the bloodsuckers. There is some amazing stunt work being done with the axe (lots of fancy axe twirling). The CG is very prominent, and I don't know that that horse stampede sequence is at all believable. Going in, I was keenly anticipating this film. The premise just made me happy. So I'm not sure what happened. Maybe Russian director Timur Bekmambetov is too mired in grand and heavy-handed mythology-building to make time and space for a wink of the eye, a nudge in the ribs. But this film could've done with a bright sense of fun. All that dramatic brooding eventually wears you down. Just as bad, the acting performances generally don't rise above the cripplingly solemn script, although I do think that Dominic Cooper turns in some pretty terrific scenes (I like his flashback story). There's also a reverse twist near the end which I didn't see coming, that I enjoyed.<br /><br />I do like the film's interpretation of the vampires. It's interesting that no vampire can kill another, that they vanish and then resurface in a terrifying blink. And with judicious dabs of sun balm, they even participate in theCivil War.<br /><br />Abe Lincoln as vampire slayer is just badasss. His action hero creds kept elevating as he relentlessly mowed down four score and seven (and more) of the undead. In fact, he comes off as so indomitable that, after a while, you stop treating him like a real character. You stop worrying about him. Ultimately, he's just one in a number of paper-thin characters inhabiting this movie. Benjamin Walker, unknown actor, has great physical presence but no gravitas. He fails to deliver on those emotional beats. He has zero chemistry with Mary Elizabeth Winstead who plays his love interest, Mary Todd. Walker doesn't really look like the Lincoln we're familiar with until around the third act. As revisionist histories go, ABRAHAM LINCOLN, VAMPIRE HUNTER falls victim to clunky storytelling and one-dimensional characterization but is buoyed by the well-executed action flourishes. There is a fun element of camp, yes, but an even stronger dose of cheekiness could've gone a long way. 3.5 out of 5 stars.Civil War.<br /><br />Abe Lincoln as vampire slayer is just badasss.
